    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1106049798" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1106049798" />
    <a title="Jill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2228181-jill"><img alt="Jill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507660688p2/2228181.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1106049798?book_show_action=true">Nov 14, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jill" href="/user/show/2228181-jill">Jill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1106049798" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15179160424914892424">Kazuo Ishiguro is a master stylist, with books that range from futuristic, sci-fi, surrealistic and post-colonial. So it is no surprise that his new novel is unlike anything he has written before. Like its predecessors, this novel concentrates on memories: their power to silence, distort, and forever haunt, with characters that are often alienated and searching. Yet the book is distinctly an adventure fable, integrating an ancient British civilization with fantasy.<br/><br/>The two questions are: is it go</span>
  <span id="freeText15179160424914892424" style="display:none">Kazuo Ishiguro is a master stylist, with books that range from futuristic, sci-fi, surrealistic and post-colonial. So it is no surprise that his new novel is unlike anything he has written before. Like its predecessors, this novel concentrates on memories: their power to silence, distort, and forever haunt, with characters that are often alienated and searching. Yet the book is distinctly an adventure fable, integrating an ancient British civilization with fantasy.<br /><br />The two questions are: is it good? And is it satisfying? The answer to the first question is absolutely yes. All the trademarks of Ishiguro – his masterful prose, his characters’ search for meaning and his feats of imagination are all well on display. The answer to the second question is far more subjective and will very much depend on what each individual reader enjoys.<br /><br />The novel takes us into post-Arthurian Britain, populated with those mythic heroes, including a still-living Sir Gawain, yet there’s a twist here: A mist has fallen on Britain, forcing the isle into a collective loss of memory. (“IT’s queer the way the world’s forgetting people and things form only yesterday and the day before that. Like a sickness come over us all.”) Axl and Beatrice (and are we sure these are even their names?) are an elderly couple who visit their adult son, of whom they have only the scantest of remembrances. During their journey, they encounter an honorable knight, a warrior on a quixotic quest, a young boy, a sinister hidden dragon, an evil monk and more, each holding a key to their quest.<br /><br />At its core, The Buried Giant is a fantastical fable, an old-fashioned adventure story with thrills a-plenty. But scratch the surface and you get more. There are allusions to so many other classics: obviously, King Arthur and the Knights of the Roundtable, but also Orpheus and Dante’s Inferno (particularly in the character of Beatrice, the cherished soulmate), and certain childhood fables.<br /><br />We also get an in-depth look at universal themes: memory and forgetfulness, love and aging, war and peace (and how peace can hold “for ever built on slaughter and a magician’s trickery” and what it takes to be a good and a loving person. From Axl to Beatrice: “For what good’s a memory’s returning from the mist if it’s only to push away another? Will you promise me, princess? Promise to keep what you feel for me this moment always in your heart, no matter what you see once the mist’s gone.” <br /><br />The narrative builds as it goes along and the central question – at what price do we remember, at what price do we forget – is captivating and its broader meaning – the collective memory loss of a nation is timely. The message is delivered within the fantasy adventure framework, which will appeal to some, but not all readers. I cannot say I loved it (as I loved Remains of the Day, Never Let Me Go, The Unconsoled and other Ishiguro works) but I admired this achievement. And I must admit to a little misting in my own eyes as the fable finally surrenders its secrets.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15179160424914892424" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2385371187" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2385371187" />
    <a title="Dolors" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18383778-dolors"><img alt="Dolors" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1502350013p2/18383778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2385371187?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dolors" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dolors" href="/user/show/18383778-dolors">Dolors</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780571315031" href="/book/show/22706570-the-buried-giant">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Not too demanding readers of fables</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18383778-dolors?shelf=read-in-2018">read-in-2018</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2385371187" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12747956097421319087">In <i>“The Buried Giant”</i> nothing is what it seems. <br/>A fable that overbrims with symbolism and allegory. <br/>A river to cross that might signify the last journey for an elderly couple on the land of the living. A tale set in post-Arthurian England with knights, dragons, ogres, sword duels and magic spells to convey aspects of human nature that elude all kind of logic, such as love, forgiveness, war and revenge.<br/><br/>Blending mythological legend and medieval epic, Ishiguro builds a world were people suffer from</span>
  <span id="freeText12747956097421319087" style="display:none">In <i>“The Buried Giant”</i> nothing is what it seems. <br />A fable that overbrims with symbolism and allegory. <br />A river to cross that might signify the last journey for an elderly couple on the land of the living. A tale set in post-Arthurian England with knights, dragons, ogres, sword duels and magic spells to convey aspects of human nature that elude all kind of logic, such as love, forgiveness, war and revenge.<br /><br />Blending mythological legend and medieval epic, Ishiguro builds a world were people suffer from general amnesia. That is the price to pay for peace and conviviality between Saxons and Britons. I wonder if many of us do use the same notion to forgive and forget what life and others have inflicted on us. <br />For Axel and Beatrice, the old married couple and protagonists of this novel, forgetfulness is no longer a solution because they ache to remember what happened to their son, the cost of peace has become too high. So they set off on a trip in search of the cause that mists their minds, hoping they’ll recover their stolen memories and the location of their son along with them.<br /><br />This could have been a great adventure story, the kind of rich parable that offers several layers of receding depth to peel off as the novel unfolds its mysteries. But in my opinion, Mr. Ishiguro failed to create a believable setting and he focused rather on making explicit the hidden metaphors, neglecting the form of the narration. And so the characters lacked essence, the dialogues were forced and full of annoying tics and the revelation was discovered too abruptly to be plausible.<br />Mr. Ishiguro is known for his ability to elude genre classification, but it’s precisely the vague quality of this novel that dashed its potentiality. Nevertheless, a few images will remain with me, such as an old couple crossing a river trusting that their true love will beat any wrongdoings they inflicted on each other. Fantasy or no fantasy, this is an undeniable ode to love, trust and memory, and so all in all, a worthwhile reading.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12747956097421319087" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText2111305374348253106" style="display:none">For the first hundred pages or so I thought I was going to love this book. The idea of the old couple setting off on what amounted to a pilgrimage, the mist and the way people were losing memories and the beautifully executed writing style all added up to a possible five star read. Then just around the half way point I suddenly realised I was a little bored and was starting to skim the longer paragraphs. Part of this was that I had developed an attachment to Axl and Beatrice and did not want to listen to the musings of an elderly knight. Towards the end when the old couple's story took the major focus again I regained my interest and was involved enough to appreciate the way events transpired. So sadly it ends up just a three star book for me, one of those stars being entirely for the beautiful way this author uses words.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1427424293" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1427424293" />
    <a title="Joey Woolfardis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4639825-joey-woolfardis"><img alt="Joey Woolfardis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1490696206p2/4639825.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1427424293?book_show_action=true">Oct 28, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joey Woolfardis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joey Woolfardis" href="/user/show/4639825-joey-woolfardis">Joey Woolfardis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780571315031" href="/book/show/22706570-the-buried-giant">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4639825-joey-woolfardis?shelf=2015">2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4639825-joey-woolfardis?shelf=bloody-cack">bloody-cack</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4639825-joey-woolfardis?shelf=sterling">sterling</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4639825-joey-woolfardis?shelf=masculine">masculine</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4639825-joey-woolfardis?shelf=ce21">ce21</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4639825-joey-woolfardis?shelf=bookshelf">bookshelf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1427424293" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8872179752613608076">This was my 900th book that I've read. Let me be clear on that, when I say my 900th book. I have not read nine hundred books from page one to the end. Or actually, I might have done. There may be books from my childhood and my pre-GoodReads-and-blog days where I read a book-either 3 pages or 500 pages long-that I've read all the way through that I've since forgotten about. As it stands, these are books that I've picked up, either physically or electronically and thought, "yes, I want to read you</span>
  <span id="freeText8872179752613608076" style="display:none">This was my 900th book that I've read. Let me be clear on that, when I say my 900th book. I have not read nine hundred books from page one to the end. Or actually, I might have done. There may be books from my childhood and my pre-GoodReads-and-blog days where I read a book-either 3 pages or 500 pages long-that I've read all the way through that I've since forgotten about. As it stands, these are books that I've picked up, either physically or electronically and thought, "yes, I want to read you". Some of them have disappointed; some I haven't managed to get beyond the first few chapters, but for me I have read them. Please feel free to have your own opinions, but I neither feel like I've cheated myself nor you. It is simply a number.<br /><br /><br /><br />But yes, my 900th book and I was very excited.<br /><br /><br /><br />There are two things I wish to point out, firstly, I am an over-bearing high-fantasy, none of your twee-YA-mild-fantasy-rubbish, fan: a true high-fantasy, heavy-on-the-world-building-high-fantasy fan, and secondly, I have never read a book by Kazuo Ishiguro nor seen any adaptations of his works. <br /><br />It sounded intriguing. His imagination is vast, much like Neil Gaiman's, and the initial storyline was very intriguing. At the beginning I was thinking maybe two stars, half-way through when Sir Gawain turned up it shifted up to a three-star rating but then it slowly sank in to the ground and pretty much died the death of a thousand dead men, who you think are dead but do that last-gasp thing and then really do die.<br /><br />I don't want to go in to a whole load of detail, so I will throw out the main areas that bugged me. Firstly, it felt like a contemporary novel with a fantasy-genre guise, written only to win Big Shiny Awards, because genre fiction, like fantasy, doesn't win Big Shiny Awards That Anyone Cares About. I'm looking at the Man Booker Prize, mostly. Secondly, the characters were 2D and fairly annoying, with exasperating dialogue. I appreciate their memory loss made them rather stale, but otherwise they were uninteresting and I had no sympathy for them, nor did I feel any empathy toward their plight.<br /><br />Thirdly, fantasy is basically The World with everything else coming in second. Fantasy is built on world-building: that's mainly the whole point of it. You don't need a whole planet, and you certainly don't need any thing made-up: Earth serves its purpose well for a fantasy novel. This book had none: "ooh, there's a forest over there" was pretty much it. At the beginning of the book we get a nice description of what England looked like back then, but that's about it. You need to remember this throughout the entire book. It's either desolate, wind-swept planes or trees.<br /><br />Fourthly, and this is a very personal opinion, but I disliked the way Sir Gawain was portrayed. Yes, he's my favourite Arthurian Knight, no, I don't know the Arthurian legends enough to really know if he was correctly portrayed, but I've read Sir Gawain and the Green Knight, and some of Lord Tennyson's work, and Sir Gawain is not an impetuous whinger, I do know that.<br /><br />A few other things I'll leave out because of spoilers, but the ideas some of the characters had were inane and self-serving, especially toward the end. In fact, the entire ending, aside from the exact end, was just a heap of disappointment. And my Lord, if Axl called Beatrice "Princess" one more time than he did I'm pretty sure I would have stopped reading. The whole thing smacked of "hey I know a secret but I'm not going to tell you 'til the end" and we didn't actually find much out at the end.<br /><br />What did I like? Sir Gawain, of course, and the weird way we sort of dropped in to an Arthurian Legend as if it were a true part of History. That was imaginative and fun, but not done well. The name of the book itself was grand, though very ill-chosen (again, with the "here's a secret I may or may not tell you about later"), and the fact it had dragons and a type of magic. Yeah, the cover.<br /><br />I don't really know. An experiment gone wrong, perhaps? This certainly hasn't stopped me from trying his other works out. I've read a few other reviews from people who have read his other works and they all seem to say the same thing: this is very different and not anywhere near as good. That makes me feel a bit better about not liking it, to be honest. I don't know why I feel bad for that: perhaps one star is too harsh, but the way it made me feel-almost angry toward the end-makes me think I can't give it any more.<br /><br />At least I read my 900th book all the way through. Every single word.<br /><br /><br /><a href="https://www.infiniteanddarling.co.uk/blogs/infinite-variety-books" rel="nofollow noopener">Blog</a> | <a href="https://www.instagram.com/infiniteANDdarling/" rel="nofollow noopener">Instagram</a> | <a href="https://twitter.com/infinitedarlin" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a> | <a href="https://uk.pinterest.com/infinitedarling/" rel="nofollow noopener">Pinterest</a> | <a href="https://www.infiniteanddarling.co.uk" rel="nofollow noopener">Shop</a> | <a href="https://www.etsy.com/uk/shop/infiniteANDdarling" rel="nofollow noopener">Etsy</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="8872179752613608076" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5096250725118123819">Spoiler-free video review: <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=DoTOXAmOy5Y'>https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=DoTOX...</a><br/><br/>Written review (also spoiler-free):<br/>Ishiguro’s new novel was my most anticipated release of this year, so I was unbelievably excited when a review copy came in from the Allen & Unwin representative. Thank goodness this lived up to (and even exceeded) my expectations. Set in 6th Century Britain, it follows an elderly couple who set out from their village to find their son. I knew nothing about this going into it, so it took me a li</span>
  <span id="freeText5096250725118123819" style="display:none">Spoiler-free video review: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=DoTOXAmOy5Y">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=DoTOX...</a><br /><br />Written review (also spoiler-free):<br />Ishiguro’s new novel was my most anticipated release of this year, so I was unbelievably excited when a review copy came in from the Allen &amp; Unwin representative. Thank goodness this lived up to (and even exceeded) my expectations. Set in 6th Century Britain, it follows an elderly couple who set out from their village to find their son. I knew nothing about this going into it, so it took me a little while to place it in the fantasy genre. However, this is fantasy in the same way that Never Let Me Go is science-fiction, so the emphasis is on the characters’ relationships and their inner lives. The fantasy elements are there to create a framework for the themes of memory and love that motivate the characters. There is a mist covering the land that causes people to forget elements of their past, so everyone is an unreliable narrator. A few typical Ishiguro traits can be found, such as the emphasis on memory and sparse but evocative description, but it is unlike anything he has written in the past. This is the kind of book that Tolkien might have written had he decided to focus on rich characterisation and relationships rather than the landscape. There are some treats for fans of British legend too, as this is set not long after the death of King Arthur.</span>

<div id="review_1229784424" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1229784424" />
    <a title="Elaine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2352900-elaine"><img alt="Elaine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505699475p2/2352900.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1229784424?book_show_action=true">Mar 17, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elaine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elaine" href="/user/show/2352900-elaine">Elaine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2352900-elaine?shelf=2015">2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1229784424" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13282411746952608033">There are certain writers - David Mitchell, Kate Atkinson, Hilary Mantel, Chiang Rae Lee - where I think, "OK, I'll follow you anywhere, no matter how weird or improbable that place you want to take me is." A Dutch enclave in 18th century Japan? A futuristic Baltimore devoted to pisciculture? 1800 pages telling Tudor history from the vantage point of one of its better known villains? Groundhog Day set in WW2? Yes, and yes, and yes, and yes, and there you have half of my favorite books from the l</span>
  <span id="freeText13282411746952608033" style="display:none">There are certain writers - David Mitchell, Kate Atkinson, Hilary Mantel, Chiang Rae Lee - where I think, "OK, I'll follow you anywhere, no matter how weird or improbable that place you want to take me is." A Dutch enclave in 18th century Japan? A futuristic Baltimore devoted to pisciculture? 1800 pages telling Tudor history from the vantage point of one of its better known villains? Groundhog Day set in WW2? Yes, and yes, and yes, and yes, and there you have half of my favorite books from the last decade. <br /><br />Ishiguro definitely holds his place in that pantheon of trust. Organ harvesting and a dystopian British boarding school? Yes! Mosleyites in an uglier (and realer) version of Downton Abbey? Yes! And so, based on that trust, I tried and tried again to give myself over to this pastiche of Arthurian legend, which has been billed as a "fantasy novel," but is told more in the limpid aphoristic tones of fable. <br /><br />But I failed. The writing, lyrical in places and clumping in others, tripped me up. The plot (such as it was) dragged, with the foreboding atmosphere a bit of an overmatch for the reveal at the end. <br /><br />The Buried Giant worked for me as concept, as meditation on memory and genocide, on love and loss. But it didn't work for me as a novel, and if Ishiguro's project was to deracinate his story so that we focused more on the concepts and less on the trappings of setting and narrative, then I think his project failed because the weird setting and characters distracted me, like a tooth that you can't stop worrying at. <br /><br />Even the greats stumble sometimes. Better to experiment and fail than get stuck in mediocrity.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13282411746952608033" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Maziar MHK" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/81103111-maziar-mhk"><img alt="Maziar MHK" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596227028p2/81103111.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer888160963762650141">گر چه بعضی جاها، حِس می کردم داستان داره یِه تِمِ رازآلود می گیره و پرشِ های متوالی به پیش و پس، چه زمانی وَچِه محتوایی رو تجربه می کنه، اما کُلاََ از اون رُمانا بود که، تو چن روزِ مطالعه، حواسَم از خطِ داستان پَرت نمی شد، حتی وقتایی که کتاب بِدست نبودم<br/>یه مکتوبِ دوست داشتنی با روایتی تئاتری و نمادگرا از فراموش کردنِ این اصلِ حیاتیِ<i> نباید فراموش کنیم که یه روزی "فراموش می کنیم" و فراموش می شویم</i> <br/><br/>:در گیراییِ دلنشینِ این رمان، همین بَس که<br/>اگر نویسنده ای باشد و کتابی، که مطالعه کتابِ مذکورِ آن نویسنده</span>
  <span id="freeText888160963762650141" style="display:none">گر چه بعضی جاها، حِس می کردم داستان داره یِه تِمِ رازآلود می گیره و پرشِ های متوالی به پیش و پس، چه زمانی وَچِه محتوایی رو تجربه می کنه، اما کُلاََ از اون رُمانا بود که، تو چن روزِ مطالعه، حواسَم از خطِ داستان پَرت نمی شد، حتی وقتایی که کتاب بِدست نبودم<br />یه مکتوبِ دوست داشتنی با روایتی تئاتری و نمادگرا از فراموش کردنِ این اصلِ حیاتیِ<i> نباید فراموش کنیم که یه روزی "فراموش می کنیم" و فراموش می شویم</i> <br /><br />:در گیراییِ دلنشینِ این رمان، همین بَس که<br />اگر نویسنده ای باشد و کتابی، که مطالعه کتابِ مذکورِ آن نویسنده، باعث شود، بقیه آثارِ آن نویسنده را فِلفور و "بی اما و اگر" بِخَری تا بخوانی، آن نویسنده "ایشی گورو" ست و آن کتاب هم، یک انتخابِ بسیار سخت، میانِ <i>"بازمانده روز"</i> از یک سو وَ <i>"غول مدفون"</i> از دیگر سو<br /><br />این را داشته باشید تا <u>"تسلی ناپذیر" و "هرگز رهایم مکن"</u> را هم بخوانم، تا باز از ایشی گورو و قلمِ دلفریبش حرف بزنیم</span>

<div id="review_1324357659" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1324357659" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41609617-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539156508p2/41609617.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1324357659?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/41609617-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780571315031" href="/book/show/22706570-the-buried-giant">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1324357659" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16494313748535913578">‘The Buried Giant’ is the first of Ishiguro's novels that I have read – and being well aware of his previous accomplishments, critical acclaim, Nobel and Booker prize winning standing – it was with much anticipation, high expectation, although inevitably some apprehension that I embarked on this book – would Ishiguro and this novel in particular live up to his high standing and reputation? I was far from disappointed… <br/><br/>‘The Buried Giant’ is a hauntingly evocative, compelling and intelligent story</span>
  <span id="freeText16494313748535913578" style="display:none">‘The Buried Giant’ is the first of Ishiguro's novels that I have read – and being well aware of his previous accomplishments, critical acclaim, Nobel and Booker prize winning standing – it was with much anticipation, high expectation, although inevitably some apprehension that I embarked on this book – would Ishiguro and this novel in particular live up to his high standing and reputation? I was far from disappointed… <br /><br />‘The Buried Giant’ is a hauntingly evocative, compelling and intelligent story set in a post-Roman, post-Arthurian Britain. Ostensibly this is a story with a ‘Fantasy’ setting – and on one level very much of that genre and whilst there are nods to and echoes of Tolkien et al, the ‘Fantasy’ setting is just that, a ‘setting’ only, providing a backdrop to the big themes that Ishiguro is exploring here.<br /><br />In one sense, ‘The Buried Giant’ is akin to an Arthurian (style) legend, a journey, a quest and all that goes with that – it does feel in some sense like a fable, a parable, a legend, mythological and elemental; referencing not only Cervantes’ ‘Don Quixote’, but the likes of Eco’s ‘Name of The Rose’.<br /><br />On another level however, Ishiguro’s novel is all about memory, loss and remembrance – it’s about the suppression of past deeds, wrongs done, the denial of past ‘evils’; it’s about the legitimisation of war and slaughter on the basis of future peace, the questioning of ‘ends justifying the means’. Confronting, rationalising and coming to terms with a sometimes ugly and difficult past.<br /><br />The narrative of ‘The Buried Giant’ is delivered by Ishiguro ostensibly on two key levels – there is the very personal story of Axl and Beatrice and their journey to find their son; the journey that they must go on and their remembrance of things past – past wrongs being remembered, righted or at least confronted; the healing of old wounds, the journey of life along with a ultimately a contemplation of death and isolation. On another level, ‘The Buried Giant’ is all about the ‘wrongs’ done at societal level, mass slaughter and genocide – the subsequent acceptance and justification of same and ultimately the rewriting of both memory and history in order to deliver an acceptable and palatable story, A process of forgetting, remembrance and rationalisation or suppression of the past. Therefore, ‘The Buried Giant’ is all about not just individual, but more importantly collective amnesia.<br /><br />‘The Buried Giant’ is a great book, so very well delivered – haunting, memorable, effective, affecting, compelling, amusing, exciting; ambitious in scope – simple yet complex, thought-provoking, evocative and intelligent – a great adventure and a great story, what more could you want? Highly recommended. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16494313748535913578" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2144360733" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2144360733" />
    <a title="Perry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6354112-perry"><img alt="Perry" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588528919p2/6354112.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2144360733?book_show_action=true">Oct 05, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Perry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Perry" href="/user/show/6354112-perry">Perry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2144360733" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12765442883164188520">The Raindrops of Our Memories<br/><br/>“But then again I wonder if what we feel in our hearts today isn't like these raindrops still falling on us from the soaked leaves above, even though the sky itself long stopped raining. I'm wondering if without our memories, there's nothing for it but for our love to fade and die.” K. Ishiguro, The Buried Giant<br/><br/>There are times, I think, one must admit bewilderment and a certain lack of knowledge or learning to fully understand some novels, but can nonetheless say tha</span>
  <span id="freeText12765442883164188520" style="display:none">The Raindrops of Our Memories<br /><br />“But then again I wonder if what we feel in our hearts today isn't like these raindrops still falling on us from the soaked leaves above, even though the sky itself long stopped raining. I'm wondering if without our memories, there's nothing for it but for our love to fade and die.” K. Ishiguro, The Buried Giant<br /><br />There are times, I think, one must admit bewilderment and a certain lack of knowledge or learning to fully understand some novels, but can nonetheless say that the story impacted him; that, in a relatively short read, he inhabited the phenomenal, yet surreal, atmosphere of dense fog that enveloped the terrain and the faded memories of the protagonist medieval married couple (and of all the population in the mystical land); that he felt for the couple and their lost memories and, most importantly, that he has a deeper understanding, from having read this book, of how much memories are a significant part of who we are and of our love for those in our lives, and yet at the same time our love is more than memories: true love is in our souls and as long as we can see and know those we love, our love will not die, in spite of our vanishing remembrances. <br /><br />I know this sounds mawkish. Yet, because I cannot articulate to you exactly why I found the story so profound, I am left to conveying my impressions, however sentimental they may seem.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12765442883164188520" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1221020704" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1221020704" />
    <a title="Maciek" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2969647-maciek"><img alt="Maciek" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1280839159p2/2969647.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1221020704?book_show_action=true">Mar 07, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maciek" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maciek" href="/user/show/2969647-maciek">Maciek</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=2015-releases">2015-releases</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=read-in-2015">read-in-2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1221020704" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6924791477571427470">Kazuo Ishiguro's latest book came as a surprise. It's been 10 years since his last novel, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6334.Never_Let_Me_Go' title='Never Let Me Go by Kazuo Ishiguro' rel='nofollow noopener'>Never Let Me Go</a>, and apart from a lone collection of short stories - <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4772110.Nocturnes_Five_Stories_of_Music_and_Nightfall' title='Nocturnes Five Stories of Music and Nightfall by Kazuo Ishiguro' rel='nofollow noopener'>Nocturnes: Five Stories of Music and Nightfall</a> in 2009 - the author has largely been silent. <i>The Buried Giant</i> appeared suddenly and unexpectedly, without much fanfare - shouldn't it be a big deal?<br/><br/>Ishiguro's latest book is very different from the two of his novels that I have read, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28921.The_Remains_of_the_Day' title='The Remains of the Day by Kazuo Ishiguro' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Remains of the Day</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6334.Never_Let_Me_Go' title='Never Let Me Go by Kazuo Ishiguro' rel='nofollow noopener'>Never Let Me Go</a> - both of which </span>
  <span id="freeText6924791477571427470" style="display:none">Kazuo Ishiguro's latest book came as a surprise. It's been 10 years since his last novel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6334.Never_Let_Me_Go" title="Never Let Me Go by Kazuo Ishiguro" rel="nofollow noopener">Never Let Me Go</a>, and apart from a lone collection of short stories - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4772110.Nocturnes_Five_Stories_of_Music_and_Nightfall" title="Nocturnes Five Stories of Music and Nightfall by Kazuo Ishiguro" rel="nofollow noopener">Nocturnes: Five Stories of Music and Nightfall</a> in 2009 - the author has largely been silent. <i>The Buried Giant</i> appeared suddenly and unexpectedly, without much fanfare - shouldn't it be a big deal?<br /><br />Ishiguro's latest book is very different from the two of his novels that I have read, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28921.The_Remains_of_the_Day" title="The Remains of the Day by Kazuo Ishiguro" rel="nofollow noopener">The Remains of the Day</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6334.Never_Let_Me_Go" title="Never Let Me Go by Kazuo Ishiguro" rel="nofollow noopener">Never Let Me Go</a> - both of which were set in contemporary, recognizable times. <i>The Buried Giant</i> takes place in the distant past - so distant that it's the stuff of myths and legends. It is set in Britain after the death of King Arthur, a country divided between Britons and Saxons. Although the wars have ended, there is a sense of aversion between the two groups - and a mist seems to have fallen over the entire land, clouding memories and perception: there is talk of monsters and mythical creatures roaming in the wilderness.<br /><br />The novel focuses on an elderly couple, Axl and Beatrice, who set out on a quest to find their son whom they both only dimly remember having. Axl and Beatrice are deeply devoted to one another - he refers to her only as "Princess" - but both can't even remember what their son looked like, as they have not seen him in many, many years. But their situation is not unique - a kind of a mist has set over the land and shrouded everything, and other villagers have similar trouble remembering details from their own lives.<br /><br />On their journey Axl and Beatrice will cross many paths, and meet several characters that will accompany them on their way, with nothing being quite what it seems. Ishiguro succeeds in creating a sense of mystery and strangeness reminiscent of folk fables and fairy tales, a opaque world surrounded with fog and dew; dreams mix with reality, and perception is not entirely reliable. <br /><br />What I had trouble with was the book's slow pace, but this might be excused; after all it might be deliberate, to signify that the novel is about transition, and one that might not even be completed, and end up as some kind of a limbo. What lost me was the introduction of a well-known figure from the Arthurian legends, who became a major character in the story. This is where I pretty much lost interest - I felt that this character took the focus away from Ishiguro's own creations, to overall detriment. <br /><br />Fantasy fans are most likely to be disappointed by <i>The Buried Giant</i> - it does feature several staples of the genre, but mostly focused on theme of selective, incomplete memory, which is present in the two other novels by him that I have read. Ironically, I found the book to be hard to remember - it's deliberately constructed to obfuscate and hide true meaning and intent, which will undoubtedly satisfy many readers but also turn away many others. As always, ultimately the best way to see which camp you fall in is to read the book yourself - but if you'll find your attention slipping away, perhaps your memory will be better served by something else. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6924791477571427470" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText1571788028930239777" style="display:none">“Who knows what will come when quick-tongued men make ancient grievances rhyme with fresh desire for land and conquest?”<br /><br /><i>“And then she asked me: ‘How will you and your husband prove your love for each other when you can’t remember the past you’ve shared?’ And I’ve been thinking about it ever since. Sometimes I think of it and it makes me so afraid.”</i><br /><br />I find the rhythm of Ishiguro's writing in this mesmerizing. And I usually despise repetition in dialogue, but repeated phrases ratchet the tension very steadily, and show the thousand kindnesses between the old couple. It's a melancholy tale that knocked around in my head for days, very much in the background though. It was a whole week later when I was ironing a shirt, and thinking about yet another conflict in the Middle East and the impact of the book hit me with a wallop -- love, war, remembrance, and our human cycles of revenge and betrayal -- how will we overcome them?</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer992726523" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6935703337103915552"><br/>Maybe Ishiguro was thinking about Queequeg and drinking a coffee from his Keurig as he named the dragon...Querig.<br/><br/>I will say: the last pages are intense, the way the endings of several of his other novels have been. Very intense. (The fact that there was a ton of foreshadowing didn't make them less intense.) However, I can't get too enthusiastic about the previous 300 pages. I didn't dislike this novel, exactly, but nor am I a fantasy reader. And Ishiguro isn't really writing a fantasy novel here</span>
  <span id="freeText6935703337103915552" style="display:none"><br />Maybe Ishiguro was thinking about Queequeg and drinking a coffee from his Keurig as he named the dragon...Querig.<br /><br />I will say: the last pages are intense, the way the endings of several of his other novels have been. Very intense. (The fact that there was a ton of foreshadowing didn't make them less intense.) However, I can't get too enthusiastic about the previous 300 pages. I didn't dislike this novel, exactly, but nor am I a fantasy reader. And Ishiguro isn't really writing a fantasy novel here; a very pared-down fantasy world just happens to be the skin in which his other ideas are living, the way a very pared-down sci-fi dystopian world was the skin for the broader ideas of <i>Never Let Me Go</i>. But there is something about dragons and pixies that makes my eyes glaze. Also I must scold the author for allowing his protagonist to call his other protagonist "princess."</span>

<div id="review_1025456046" class="review notext nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1025456046" />
    <a title="Daniel Kosack" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29568788-daniel-kosack"><img alt="Daniel Kosack" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1394629971p2/29568788.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniel Kosack" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniel Kosack" href="/user/show/29568788-daniel-kosack">Daniel Kosack</a>
      </span>

<div id="review_1080350802" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1080350802" />
    <a title="Taryn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13977687-taryn"><img alt="Taryn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591322913p2/13977687.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1080350802?book_show_action=true">Oct 14, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Taryn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Taryn" href="/user/show/13977687-taryn">Taryn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1080350802" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3901120839171103884">I read Ishiguro's fantabulously gorgeous <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6334.Never_Let_Me_Go' title='Never Let Me Go by Kazuo Ishiguro' rel='nofollow noopener'>Never Let Me Go</a> during a brief and ill-advised stint in grad school, and it was one of the few assigned books I actually enjoyed. So when I heard he was publishing a new book this month, I made sure I was at the top of the library hold list. <br/><br/>And I'm glad I got a paper copy, because the hardcover version is lovely. The dust jacket feels like fabric instead of paper, and the gold-accented cover art fits the cryptic, mysterious feel of the book. The library </span>
  <span id="freeText3901120839171103884" style="display:none">I read Ishiguro's fantabulously gorgeous <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6334.Never_Let_Me_Go" title="Never Let Me Go by Kazuo Ishiguro" rel="nofollow noopener">Never Let Me Go</a> during a brief and ill-advised stint in grad school, and it was one of the few assigned books I actually enjoyed. So when I heard he was publishing a new book this month, I made sure I was at the top of the library hold list. <br /><br />And I'm glad I got a paper copy, because the hardcover version is lovely. The dust jacket feels like fabric instead of paper, and the gold-accented cover art fits the cryptic, mysterious feel of the book. The library clerk who checked me out even took a minute to look it over; she hadn't heard of Ishiguro, but upon inspecting his author photograph pronounced him “a good-looking man.” <br /><br />Even though he could skate by on his looks, Ishiguro delivers another lush, lyrical novel, this time set in a strange, parallel-universe Britain in the Middle Ages (though thanks to some specific linguistic choices, the story has the timeless feel of a fable). Axl and Beatrice, an elderly couple, set out from their village to visit their son. Though it should be a straightforward journey, they find their way fraught with confusion and peril. The presence of a she-dragon in the region casts a mist over everyone's memories, making it a constant struggle for the couple to remember basic information such as how they met and why their son went away. <br /><br />Axl and Beatrice are dear despite their befuddlement. Right away I liked their tender way of speaking to each other; even as it grew repetitive, it never grated. I hoped for a good ending for them, although I wasn't sure they would get one—the occasional flashes of memory they do regain are ominous, full of old hurts and grievances, the emotions of which are more vivid than the facts. It seems unlikely they will come to their son's village and find him happily awaiting them.<br /><br />I've seen some reviews that take issue with the opacity of the novel, and it's true that Ishiguro obscures almost as much from the reader as he does from his characters. Much is left unsaid and unexplained. But his prose is so fracking gorgeous, I was hard pressed to notice—much less be bothered by—anything else. His writing is as plush as an old armchair. I want to sink in and never find my way back out. <br /><br />It may not be as clearly realized as Never Let Me Go, but The Buried Giant is still some nose-scrunchingly good literary fiction. I was *this close* to rubbing my face on the pages.<br /><br />More book recommendations by me at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.readingwithhippos.com">www.readingwithhippos.com</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="3901120839171103884" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1088141938" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1088141938" />
    <a title="Kyle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3974974-kyle"><img alt="Kyle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1410285114p2/3974974.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1088141938?book_show_action=true">Oct 24, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kyle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kyle" href="/user/show/3974974-kyle">Kyle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1088141938" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9060740289074178228">We all have groupie moments in our life. I've heard many a couple say throughout the years that they have a "pass" established with their partner for a certain celebrity or athlete. Some of the most potent personal desires on my "fuck me" list are authors. I say all this to convey just how much I am down with Kazuo Ishiguro. Seriously. Panties on the FLOOR. He might be able to get me in bed with writing technical jargon.<br/><br/>This man can write so beautifully that it makes me jealous when I read his w</span>
  <span id="freeText9060740289074178228" style="display:none">We all have groupie moments in our life. I've heard many a couple say throughout the years that they have a "pass" established with their partner for a certain celebrity or athlete. Some of the most potent personal desires on my "fuck me" list are authors. I say all this to convey just how much I am down with Kazuo Ishiguro. Seriously. Panties on the FLOOR. He might be able to get me in bed with writing technical jargon.<br /><br />This man can write so beautifully that it makes me jealous when I read his work. Ultimately I am thankful for it though because when I read his novels I feel like I am reading some of the best crafted sentences that there are. I feel like I am truly reviewing a legitimate artist's work, and that is super cool. Throughout The Buried Giant, I came across several passages that were written with so much elegance that they gave me chills, and I was reminded why I love him so much. At the end of the day that is why I love to read, and I would say that is the best reason why one should complete The Buried Giant. <br /><br />Was this my favorite Ishiguro novel? No. Nothing may ever top Never Let Me Go. But, I did like this. It was just not what I expected. When I heard that Ishiguro was writing a new piece I avoided reading any details on purpose. When I actually got to dive in, I was completely taken off guard by the characters and plot. And for some reason, I was never able to shake that the entire time. Regardless, it doesn't matter...he is worth it. <br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="9060740289074178228" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1091375532" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7362549387352603710">For someone who has read all of Ishiguro's books (and has great respect for him), this one didn’t work for me. Although it has a promising start, with its focus on lost memories, I found the first half of the novel deadly dull. Although I found the second half far more readable, the thematic issues were insufficiently presented to make it work as a literary novel (and I no longer read stories about knights and dragons). There are moments where one encounters the wonderful strangeness Ishiguro br</span>
  <span id="freeText7362549387352603710" style="display:none">For someone who has read all of Ishiguro's books (and has great respect for him), this one didn’t work for me. Although it has a promising start, with its focus on lost memories, I found the first half of the novel deadly dull. Although I found the second half far more readable, the thematic issues were insufficiently presented to make it work as a literary novel (and I no longer read stories about knights and dragons). There are moments where one encounters the wonderful strangeness Ishiguro brings to his worlds, but those moments were too few for me.</span>

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground">Readers also enjoyed</h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"><div class='bookCarousel'>
<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>
<ul>
<li class='cover' id='bookCover_55337419'>
<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/55337419-evler-cinler-perdeler"><img alt="Evler, Cinler, Perdeler" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1600194057l/55337419._SX98_.jpg" />
</a>

  
<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('4ufSEzuQUqc+oVJ4+LzufPMfdlekOYwUDPLteUlzSQQJ33I6RyWlh05aY6nnUsNFZxZuIYpreUCiDeeZrnyrOQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('IWiYKc+N39qzB0oKS01rs1Q3sQTp9WPIYY0hpSI7UoDKUDgAszgo+sP8e9tUo0aKwD6pcsenlpzPcitFxTSwvQ==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Never far from his closest companion—a stray mule named Valentina whom he is determined to keep for himself after the war—Juan engages in the low-brow drinking escapades, long shots at love, and an otherwise droning existence shared by his compatriots.\n\nAs he lies, cheats, and steals to protect Valentina during his improbable odyssey home, Juan unwittingly “fights” for both sides—and becomes a       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4280.Kazuo_Ishiguro">About Kazuo Ishiguro</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4280.Kazuo_Ishiguro"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1424906625p3/4280.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9949063521400203111">Sir Kazuo Ishiguro (カズオ・イシグロ or 石黒 一雄), OBE, FRSA, FRSL is a British novelist of Japanese origin and Nobel Laureate in Literature (2017). His family moved to England in 1960. Ishiguro obtained his Bachelor's degree from the University of Kent in 1978 and his Master's from the University of East Anglia's creative writing course in 1980. He became a British citizen in 1982. He now lives in London.<br/><br/>Hi</span>
  <span id="freeText9949063521400203111" style="display:none">Sir Kazuo Ishiguro (カズオ・イシグロ or 石黒 一雄), OBE, FRSA, FRSL is a British novelist of Japanese origin and Nobel Laureate in Literature (2017). His family moved to England in 1960. Ishiguro obtained his Bachelor's degree from the University of Kent in 1978 and his Master's from the University of East Anglia's creative writing course in 1980. He became a British citizen in 1982. He now lives in London.<br /><br />His first novel, <i>A Pale View of Hills</i>, won the 1982 Winifred Holtby Memorial Prize. His second novel, <i>An Artist of the Floating World</i>, won the 1986 Whitbread Prize. Ishiguro received the 1989 Man Booker prize for his third novel <i>The Remains of the Day</i>. His fourth novel, <i>The Unconsoled</i>, won the 1995 Cheltenham Prize. His latest novel is <i>The Buried Giant</i>, a New York Times bestseller. He was awarded the Nobel Prize in Literature 2017.<br /><br />His novels <i>An Artist of the Floating World</i> (1986), <i>When We Were Orphans</i> (2000), and <i>Never Let Me Go</i> (2005) were all shortlisted for the Man Booker Prize.<br /><br />In 2008, The Times ranked Ishiguro 32nd on their list of "The 50 Greatest British Writers Since 1945". In 2017, the Swedish Academy awarded him the Nobel Prize in Literature, describing him in its citation as a writer "who, in novels of great emotional force, has uncovered the abyss beneath our illusory sense of connection with the world".</span>
  <a data-text-id="9949063521400203111" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
